<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

In this approach, sensor information is carried through the modulation frequency of RFID. The aim of this work is to investigate the sensor concept and to characterize the sensor performance both theoretically and experimentally. Furthermore, the operation of the sensor RF parts and oscillator are described analytically, and the equations are verified by simulations and measurements. The concept is experimentally demonstrated at a single carrier frequency to test its suitability for ultra-high-frequency RFID applications, and shown to be feasible for implementing sensors that can be read across distances up to 14 m.</div>

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Ultra-High frequency (UHF) and microwave RF identification (RFID) enables identifying distant objects equipped with an RFID tag <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. The technology has become increasingly ubiquitous, including applications such as asset tracking and inventory in logistics, access control, and road toll collection. In addition to existing applications, there is a huge need for an inexpensive technology capable of both identification (ID) and of sensing some external parameter, such as pressure, temperature, or strain. Battery-powered active or semi-passive wireless sensors are often available, but they may be too expensive or bulky for many applications. In addition, their life-time or environmental operating conditions can be limited by the battery. These limitations can be avoided by using passive wireless sensors.</p><p>Communication methods used by passive wireless sensors can be divided into analog and digital ones. Digital sensors are also known as RFID and can further be divided into two sub-categories, depending on whether the sensor read-out is analog or digital <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>.</p><p>RFID tags equipped with integrated circuits (ICs) enable several sophisticated features such as ID, rewritable memory, and anti-collision protocol. UHF RFID has been used for sensing by adding an A/D converter, a microprocessor, or other digital logic and a sensing element to the tag <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>. In this digital sensing approach, an analog sensor value is transformed into digital format and communicated to the reader device using the digitally modulated backscattering principle. The advantage of this approach is that it facilitates a generic sensor element and is thus well suited for a very broad range of applications. However, increased power consumption due to the additional electronics reduces the read-out range significantly. As an example, sensors presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a> provide a read-out range of 1.5 m while RFID tags without a digital sensor provide a range in the order of 15 m. Therefore, a long read-out distance of UHF RFID sensor tags necessitates an on-board battery or other energy source.</p><p>Examples of RFIDs equipped with analog sensors are presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_1">[12]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_1">[13]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a>. In this approach, the RFID antenna impedance (and thus the matching frequency) is made sensitive to a measured quantity, such as moisture, by loading the antenna with a moisture-sensitive element. The sensed quantity can be obtained in two ways. In one, the reader interrogates the sensor at different frequencies and records either relative sensitivity or modulated radar cross section of the tag as a function of frequency. The sensed quantity can be related to these responses through antenna matching. The second way of obtaining the sensed quantity is to add a tuning circuit to the RFID chip. This tuning circuit is used to find the input impedance that maximizes the power transfer between the reader and the tag.</p><p>The advantage of using the antenna as a sensor is a relatively long read-out distance because the sensing element does not necessarily increase the power consumption. However, the concept requires a sensing element operating at microwave frequencies. Typical off-the-shelf sensors are not feasible because they are often designed to operate at frequencies below a few megahertz. Furthermore, the antenna impedance also changes due to proximity to conductive or dielectric objects, which can falsify the sensor read-out <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_1">[15]</a>.</p><p>Sensors utilizing analog communication are surface acoustic wave (SAW) RFID, intermodulation sensors, and resonance sensors. SAW sensors <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_1">[16]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_1">[17]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_1">[18]</a> utilize an interdigital transducer patterned on a piezoelectric substrate to convert the electromagnetic energy into a surface-acoustic wave, which is then manipulated with acoustic reflectors, transformed back to electromagnetic energy, and radiated back to the reader device. The measured quantity affects the propagation properties of the SAW on the piezoelectric substrate. The need for a piezoelectric material for the sensing element limits possible applications. In addition, SAW tags only enable hard-coded ID.</p><p>Resonance sensors (e.g., <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_1">[19]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_1">[20]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_1">[21]</a>) consist of a simple resonance circuit whose resonance is sensitive to a measured quantity. These sensors require near-field coupling to the reader, which limits their read-out distance to a few centimeters. Another hindrance is that their resonance may be affected by proximity to conductive or dielectric objects.</p><p>Recently developed intermodulation sensors utilize frequency conversion for sensor read-out <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_1">[22]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_1">[23]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_1">[24]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref25" id="context_ref_25_1">[25]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_1">[26]</a>. In this principle, the sensor is interrogated by two closely located frequencies, and the sensor responds at an intermodulation frequency. This approach enables wireless read-out of a generic sensor element, such as a microelectromechanical systems (MEMS) sensor across a very large distance (even tens of meters). The sensors can also be equipped with a hard-coded ID. Intermodulation sensors can be made insensitive to antenna proximity effects.</p><p>The properties of passive wireless sensors are summarized in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ I</a>. No single technology can simultaneously provide ID, anti-collision protocol, memory, insensitivity to antenna proximity effects, and large interrogation distance. In this paper, we present a passive wireless sensor principle potentially capable of offering all these benefits. The sensor utilizes the digital communication principle of RFID, and the sensor information is coded into the digital modulation frequency of the backscattered signal. It complies with the frequency regulations set for UHF RFID in the ISO/IEC18000-6C standard and can be compatible with existing high-end readers capable of recording the modulation frequency of the tag with sufficient precision (e.g., Infinity 510 manufactured by Sirit or Tagformance Lite by Voyantic). Compatibility with some existing RFID hardware offers substantial cost benefits.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TableÂ I </b>Summary of passive wireless sensors<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>Previously, the idea of converting sensor information to the modulation frequency of a backscatter transponder has been presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_1">[27]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_1">[28]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref29" id="context_ref_29_1">[29]</a>, but the concept is not analyzed in detail. Moreover, analytical equations for predicting the sensor performance have not been developed yet. Thus, the aim of the current work is to analyze the sensor principle and to develop analytical equations for predicting the sensor performance. In addition, equations for the sensor RF parts and oscillator are derived and demonstrated in practice. Finally, the analytically predicted sensor performance is compared with measurement results. Digital circuitry of RFID (such as memory, analog-to-digital converter, or envelope detector) is beyond the scope of the current work. However, the oscillator and sensing element described in this paper could replace the local oscillator in the digital circuitry of a conventional RFID tag. As a consequence, the remaining digital part would consume less power.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Analytical Model for the Sensor</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. Operation Principle of the Sensor</h3><p>An RFID reader communicates with the tag using a binary amplitude modulation. The tag rectifies the required energy for its operation from the interrogation signal of the reader, and replies to the reader by reflecting a portion of the signal carrying a binary modulation back to the reader. The sensor proposed in this paper operates in a similar way as a normal UHF RFID tag, except that the modulation frequency of the tag carries sensor information. According to the ISO/IEC 18000-6C standard, the modulation frequency of the tag is set by the reader device at the beginning of the communication, and it can be from 40 to 640 kHz <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref30" id="context_ref_30_2a">[30]</a>. The modulation frequency of the tag can vary <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\pm}{\hbox{4}},\ldots, {\hbox{22}}\hbox{\%}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-2"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-3"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-4"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-5" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Â±</span></span></span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-6"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-7"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-8"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-9"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-10" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">4</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-11" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">,</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-12" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.183em;">â¦</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-13" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.183em;">,</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-14" style="padding-left: 0.183em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-15"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-16"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-17"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-18" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">22</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-19"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-20"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-21" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">%</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-1">{\pm}{\hbox{4}},\ldots, {\hbox{22}}\hbox{\%}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> from the initially set frequency depending on the frequency and operating conditions. This enables coding sensor information to the modulation frequency.</p><p>The sensor can be realized by modifying the internal oscillator of an RFID chip in such a way that its oscillation frequency tunes with the measured quantity. This can be done by loading the oscillator with an inductive, capacitive, or resistive sensor element. An advantage of this principle is that generic sensor elements, such as MEMS sensors, can be used because the sensor is read out at low kilohertz frequencies. The sensing element does not necessarily increase the power consumption of the tag, and the principle therefore offers a long read-out distance. The sensor circuitry is also fully isolated from the antenna, making the concept insensitive to antenna proximity effects. This concept can potentially be made compatible with the existing RFID systems, and many sensing elements can be incorporated into one RFID tag with digitally controlled switches. The sensing elements can be arranged so that the oscillation frequency is affected by only one element at a time <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_2a">[15]</a>.</p><p>The main RF parts of the sensor (excluding the antenna) are voltage rectifier, oscillator, and modulator. The rectifier produces dc voltage for the oscillator from the received interrogation signal. When the oscillator turns on, it produces an ac output, which drives the modulator. As a consequence, a modulated signal is scattered back. In the studied case, the rectifier and modulator are the same element. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 1</a> shows a simplified RF circuit of the sensor. In the following, we present different parts of the sensor in more detail and derive analytical equations to describe their electrical behavior.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 1. </b><fig><p>Simplified RF circuit of the sensor.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Voltage Rectifier</h3><p>The rectifier is based on two Schottky diodes and a capacitor. This rectifier topology is also known as a voltage doubler <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref31" id="context_ref_31_2b">[31]</a>. A voltage doubler circuit is selected instead of a voltage quadrupler or <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">2N</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-22" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-23"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-24" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-25" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">N<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">2N</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-fold voltage multiplier because it is relatively simple to realize in practice with discrete components. The antenna is matched to the rectifier at the carrier frequency, and RF chokes are used to prevent the RF signal from dissipating in the oscillator ports. A dc block capacitor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(C_{{b}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-26" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-27"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-28" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-29"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.084em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1000.78em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-30" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">C<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.724em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-31"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-32"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-33"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-34"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-35" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">b</span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-36" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">(C_{{b}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is placed between the diode and antenna to prevent rectified voltage from grounding through the antenna, and a coupling capacitor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(C_{{p}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-37" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-38"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-39" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-40"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.144em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1000.78em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-41" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">C<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.724em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-42"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-43"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-44"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-45"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-46" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">p</span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-47" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-4">(C_{{p}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is used to ground the RF signal. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> shows the electrical equivalent circuit of the rectifier. The antenna and matching circuit with a dc block capacitor are represented as a voltage source with an internal impedance of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{{a}} = R_{{a}} + \jmath X_{{a}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-5-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-5">Z_{{a}} = R_{{a}} + \jmath X_{{a}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and the dc load of the oscillator is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{L}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-6">R_{{L}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 2. </b><fig><p>Electrical equivalent circuit of the antenna, rectifier, and modulator. The oscillator is represented with a resistor at its dc input and an ac voltage source at its output.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>In the following, we derive an equation for the rectified dc voltage across the load <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_L</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">R_L</script></tex-math></inline-formula> under small-signal conditions. The rectified voltage is later needed to predict the sensitivity of the sensor (i.e., the lowest RF power that turns the sensor on) and to predict the modulated radar cross section of the sensor.</p><p>When the sensor is illuminated by the reader device, the antenna produces a voltage <disp-formula id="deqn1" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{a}=2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-8">V_{a}=2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">V_{a}=2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{{\rm in}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-9">P_{{\rm in}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the received power, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{a}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-10-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-10">R_{{a}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the antenna resistance, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\omega_{0}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-11-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-11">\omega_{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the angular frequency. Assume that dissipations in the series resistance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{s}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-12-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-12">R_{{s}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the diodes are small compared to those in the junction resistance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{j}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-13-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-13">R_{{j}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The Schottky diode is zero biased (possible self-biasing is neglected for simplicity), and its junction resistance is given as <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{j}}= 1/(\alpha I_{{s}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-14-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-14">R_{{j}}= 1/(\alpha I_{{s}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{{s}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-15-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-15">I_{{s}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the saturation current and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\alpha = q/nkT</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-16">\alpha = q/nkT</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">q</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-17-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-17">q</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the elementary charge, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">n</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-18-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-18">n</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is an ideality factor, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">k</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-19-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-19">k</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the Boltzmann constant, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-20-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-20">T</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is temperature. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> depicts that at the time of rectification, two diodes (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">D_{1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-21-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-21">D_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">D_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-22-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-22">D_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>) produce two different junction voltages <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-23-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-23">V_{{j}1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-24-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-24">V_{{j}2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, respectively. For the sake of simplicity we calculate the rectifier dc voltage for each diode one at a time and sum the results later to obtain the total rectified dc voltage. In dc analysis, the junction capacitance can be neglected and the diode is represented as <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{j}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-25-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-25">R_{{j}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The junction voltage across diode 1 is <disp-formula id="deqn2" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}1} = 2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}} S_{{ja}1}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-26-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-26">V_{{j}1} = 2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}} S_{{ja}1}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">V_{{j}1} = 2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}}R_{{a}}} S_{{ja}1}\cos(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{{ja}1}=V_{{j}1}/V_{{a}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-27">S_{{ja}1}=V_{{j}1}/V_{{a}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the voltage transfer function from the antenna to diode 1. The small-signal approximation for junction currents under zero bias is <disp-formula id="deqn3" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I(V_{{j}})\approx I_{{s}}+ I_{{s}} \alpha V_{{j}}+{(I_{{s}} \alpha^2)\over 2}V_{{j}}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(3)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-28-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-28">I(V_{{j}})\approx I_{{s}}+ I_{{s}} \alpha V_{{j}}+{(I_{{s}} \alpha^2)\over 2}V_{{j}}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(3)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">I(V_{{j}})\approx I_{{s}}+ I_{{s}} \alpha V_{{j}}+{(I_{{s}} \alpha^2)\over 2}V_{{j}}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(3)}}</span></span></disp-formula>The linear term of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-29-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-29">V_{{j}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents the linear resistance and the square term of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-30-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-30">V_{{j}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> contributes to rectification. Let us substitute <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn2" href="#deqn2" class="fulltext-link">(2)</a> into the last term of <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn3" href="#deqn3" class="fulltext-link">(3)</a>. The dc current is <disp-formula id="deqn4" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} = 2I_{s} \alpha^2 P_{{\rm in}} R_{{a}} S_{{ja}1}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(4)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-31-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-31">I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} = 2I_{s} \alpha^2 P_{{\rm in}} R_{{a}} S_{{ja}1}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(4)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} = 2I_{s} \alpha^2 P_{{\rm in}} R_{{a}} S_{{ja}1}^2. \eqno{\hbox{(4)}}</span></span></disp-formula>This current can be represented by an equivalent current generator in parallel with the junction. The dc voltage produced by diode 1 across the load <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{L}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-32-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-32">R_{{L}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (i.e., the supply voltage for the oscillator) can be expressed as <disp-formula id="deqn5" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{1({\rm DC})} = I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-33-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-33">V_{1({\rm DC})} = I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">V_{1({\rm DC})} = I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</span></span></disp-formula>Following the same procedure for diode 2 and adding the contribution from both diodes, the overall rectified dc voltage is given as <disp-formula id="deqn6" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{\rm DC}} = \left(I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} + I_{2({j},{\rm DC})}\right) \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-34-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-34">V_{{\rm DC}} = \left(I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} + I_{2({j},{\rm DC})}\right) \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">V_{{\rm DC}} = \left(I_{1({j}, {\rm DC})} + I_{2({j},{\rm DC})}\right) \left({R_{{j}}R_{{L}}\over 2R_{{j}}+R_{{L}}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(6)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c"><h3>C. Oscillator</h3><p>A Colpitts oscillator topology is selected because it is relatively simple to analyze and realize in practice with discrete components. However, other classical oscillator topologies could be used as well. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3(a)</a> shows a Colpitts oscillator circuit without a biasing network. In the proposed concept, sensing is realized in two ways: either replacing <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-35-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-35">C_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> or <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">C_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with a capasitive sensor, or by connecting a capacitive sensitive element in parallel or in series with either of the capacitors. Possible losses of the sensing element can be embedded into <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{C}1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-37">G_{{C}1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> or <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{C}2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-38">G_{{C}2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, depending on the chosen configuration.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 3. </b><fig><p>(a) Colpitts oscillator circuit without bias network. (b) Small-signal electrical equivalent circuit of colpitts oscillator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Small-signal analysis is used to evaluate the startup condition and to calculate the oscillation frequency. Let us represent the Colpitts oscillator with the small-signal equivalent circuit shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3(b)</a>. In the following, we derive equations for the oscillation frequency, turn-on dc voltage, and output ac voltage.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec2c1"><h4>1. Oscillation Frequency and Condition</h4><p>The Kirchhoff equations for the two voltage nodes of the circuit in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3(b)</a> can be expressed in matrix format as <disp-formula id="" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">[{Y}][{V}] = 0</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-39">[{Y}][{V}] = 0</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">[{Y}][{V}] = 0</span></span></disp-formula>in which <disp-formula id="" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{[{Y}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{\jmath \omega C_{2}\!+\! G_{{C}2} \!+\! G_{{i}} \!+\! Y_{{L}} &amp; -Y_{{L}} \cr g_{{m}} \!-\! Y_{{L}} &amp; \jmath \omega C_{1}\!+\!G_{{C}1} \!+\!Y_{{L}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}}}\right] \cr [{V}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{V_{1}\cr V_{2}}\right] }</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-40-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-40">\eqalignno{[{Y}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{\jmath \omega C_{2}\!+\! G_{{C}2} \!+\! G_{{i}} \!+\! Y_{{L}} &amp; -Y_{{L}} \cr g_{{m}} \!-\! Y_{{L}} &amp; \jmath \omega C_{1}\!+\!G_{{C}1} \!+\!Y_{{L}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}}}\right] \cr [{V}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{V_{1}\cr V_{2}}\right] }</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{[{Y}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{\jmath \omega C_{2}\!+\! G_{{C}2} \!+\! G_{{i}} \!+\! Y_{{L}} &amp; -Y_{{L}} \cr g_{{m}} \!-\! Y_{{L}} &amp; \jmath \omega C_{1}\!+\!G_{{C}1} \!+\!Y_{{L}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}}}\right] \cr [{V}] &amp; = \left[\matrix{V_{1}\cr V_{2}}\right] }</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{i}} = r_{{i}}^{-1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-41">G_{{i}} = r_{{i}}^{-1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the transistor input admittance, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">g_{{m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-42-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-42">g_{{m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the transconductance, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{C}1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-43-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-43">G_{{C}1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{C}2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-44-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-44">G_{{C}2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represent the conductance of capacitors <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_1</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-45-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-45">C_1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-46-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-46">C_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, respectively. The feedback inductor admittance is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Y_{{L}}= (\jmath \omega L_{1}+ G_{{R}_{{L}}})^{-1}, G_{{R}_{{LM}}}= R_{{LM}}^{-1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-47-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-47">Y_{{L}}= (\jmath \omega L_{1}+ G_{{R}_{{L}}})^{-1}, G_{{R}_{{LM}}}= R_{{LM}}^{-1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{{LM}}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-48-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-48">R_{{{LM}}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the load resistance. Oscillation is possible when the determinant of the admittance matrix equals zero. By requiring this condition, we get an equation for the oscillation frequency <disp-formula id="deqn7" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f = {1\over 2\pi} \sqrt{{G_{{i}} g_{{m}} G_{{R}_{{L}}} G_{{R}_{{LM}}}G_{{C}1}G_{{C}2} \over C_{1} C_{2}+C_{1} L_{1}+C_{2} L_{1}}} \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-49-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-49">f = {1\over 2\pi} \sqrt{{G_{{i}} g_{{m}} G_{{R}_{{L}}} G_{{R}_{{LM}}}G_{{C}1}G_{{C}2} \over C_{1} C_{2}+C_{1} L_{1}+C_{2} L_{1}}} \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">f = {1\over 2\pi} \sqrt{{G_{{i}} g_{{m}} G_{{R}_{{L}}} G_{{R}_{{LM}}}G_{{C}1}G_{{C}2} \over C_{1} C_{2}+C_{1} L_{1}+C_{2} L_{1}}} \eqno{\hbox{(7)}}</span></span></disp-formula>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 4. </b><fig><p>Actual and modeled drain current waveform of the transistor <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref32" id="context_ref_32_2c1">[32]</a>.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
and an equation for the oscillation condition <disp-formula id="deqn8" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{&amp;\left[{C_{1}(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}}\!+\!G_{{C}2}) \!+\!C_{2}(G_{{R}_{{LM}}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}} \!+\!G_{{C}2})\over(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}} \!+\!g_{m}\!+\!G_{{C}1}\!+\!G_{{C}2})}\right] \geq {L_{1}}.\cr &amp;&amp;\hbox{(8)} }</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-50-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-50">\eqalignno{&amp;\left[{C_{1}(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}}\!+\!G_{{C}2}) \!+\!C_{2}(G_{{R}_{{LM}}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}} \!+\!G_{{C}2})\over(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}} \!+\!g_{m}\!+\!G_{{C}1}\!+\!G_{{C}2})}\right] \geq {L_{1}}.\cr &amp;&amp;\hbox{(8)} }</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{&amp;\left[{C_{1}(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}}\!+\!G_{{C}2}) \!+\!C_{2}(G_{{R}_{{LM}}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{L}}} \!+\!G_{{C}2})\over(G_{{i}}\!+\!G_{{R}_{{LM}}} \!+\!g_{m}\!+\!G_{{C}1}\!+\!G_{{C}2})}\right] \geq {L_{1}}.\cr &amp;&amp;\hbox{(8)} }</span></span></disp-formula><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c2"><h4>2. Oscillator Output Voltage</h4><p>In the oscillator circuit, a capacitive feedback is formed by capacitors <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-51-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-51">C_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-52-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-52">C_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The output voltage of the oscillator is derived from the time-domain waveform of the drain current <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I(t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-53-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-53">I(t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 4</a>. For output voltage calculation, the periodic drain current is modeled by a square wave with a period of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T_0={2\pi}/{\omega_0}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-54-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-54">T_0={2\pi}/{\omega_0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and amplitude <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_0</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-55-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-55">I_0</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The maximum drain current occurs when the gate voltage reaches its peak value. Assuming that the amplitude of the output oscillating signal is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-56-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-56">A</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the maximum drain current <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{1}(t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-57-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-57">I_{1}(t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> can be approximated when the transistor operates in the nonsaturated region <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref32" id="context_ref_32_2c2">[32]</a> <disp-formula id="deqn9" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\displaylines{I_{0} =\mu_{{n}} C_{{\rm ox}}{W\over L} \left[(V_{{\rm DC}} + A + nA - V_{t}) (V_{{\rm DC}}-A)\vphantom{{1\over 2}}\right.\hfill\cr \hfill\left. -{1\over 2}(V_{{\rm DC}}-A)^2\right] \quad{\hbox{(9)}}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-58-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-58">\displaylines{I_{0} =\mu_{{n}} C_{{\rm ox}}{W\over L} \left[(V_{{\rm DC}} + A + nA - V_{t}) (V_{{\rm DC}}-A)\vphantom{{1\over 2}}\right.\hfill\cr \hfill\left. -{1\over 2}(V_{{\rm DC}}-A)^2\right] \quad{\hbox{(9)}}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\displaylines{I_{0} =\mu_{{n}} C_{{\rm ox}}{W\over L} \left[(V_{{\rm DC}} + A + nA - V_{t}) (V_{{\rm DC}}-A)\vphantom{{1\over 2}}\right.\hfill\cr \hfill\left. -{1\over 2}(V_{{\rm DC}}-A)^2\right] \quad{\hbox{(9)}}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">n = (C_{1}+C_{2})/C_{1}, \mu_{{n}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-59-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-59">n = (C_{1}+C_{2})/C_{1}, \mu_{{n}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the mobility of electrons in the channel and the capacitance per unit area is denoted by <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{{\rm ox}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-60-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-60">C_{{\rm ox}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The MOSFET channel width, length, and threshold voltage are expressed as <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">W, L,</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-61-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-61">W, L,</script></tex-math></inline-formula>and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{t}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-62-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-62">V_{{t}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, respectively. From the Fourier series <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{1} (t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-63-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-63">I_{1} (t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the fundamental current component is given by <disp-formula id="deqn10" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{1}(t)\vert_{{\rm fund}}\approx{2\over\pi}I_{0}\sin(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-64-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-64">I_{1}(t)\vert_{{\rm fund}}\approx{2\over\pi}I_{0}\sin(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">I_{1}(t)\vert_{{\rm fund}}\approx{2\over\pi}I_{0}\sin(\omega_{0}t) \eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span></span></disp-formula>and the fundamental voltage amplitude can be expressed as <disp-formula id="deqn11" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">A\approx{2\over \pi}I_{0} R_{{LM}}. \eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-65-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-65">A\approx{2\over \pi}I_{0} R_{{LM}}. \eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">A\approx{2\over \pi}I_{0} R_{{LM}}. \eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span></span></disp-formula>From <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn10" href="#deqn10" class="fulltext-link">(10)</a> and <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn11" href="#deqn11" class="fulltext-link">(11)</a>, a simplified expression of the oscillator output voltage is given by <disp-formula id="deqn12" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{0}= {V_{{\rm DC}}\over 2\pi}\left({1\over 2} + {C_{2}\over C_{1}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-66-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-66">V_{0}= {V_{{\rm DC}}\over 2\pi}\left({1\over 2} + {C_{2}\over C_{1}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">V_{0}= {V_{{\rm DC}}\over 2\pi}\left({1\over 2} + {C_{2}\over C_{1}}\right). \eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p></div></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2d"><h3>D. Modulator</h3><p>The sensor utilizes the modulated backscattering principle for communication. Modulated backscattering is realized by applying the oscillator output voltage across the rectifier, which is also used as the modulator. The ac output voltage of the oscillator modulates the RF impedance of the rectifier. As a consequence, there are sidebands in the signal reflected from the sensor. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> shows the electrical equivalent circuit that is used to derive an equation for the signal reflected from the sensor at one modulation sideband.</p><p>Unlike in rectification, nonlinear junction capacitance contributes to the mixing due to higher frequencies considered, and it therefore needs to be taken into account. The voltage-dependent junction capacitance of the diode is given as <disp-formula id="deqn13" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{{j}}(V_{{j}}) = {C_{{j}0}\over\left(1-{V_{{j}}\over\Phi}\right)^\gamma} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-67-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-67">C_{{j}}(V_{{j}}) = {C_{{j}0}\over\left(1-{V_{{j}}\over\Phi}\right)^\gamma} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">C_{{j}}(V_{{j}}) = {C_{{j}0}\over\left(1-{V_{{j}}\over\Phi}\right)^\gamma} \eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\gamma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-68-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-68">\gamma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the profile parameter for the depletion capacitance (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\gamma=0.5</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-69-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-69">\gamma=0.5</script></tex-math></inline-formula> for a uniformly doped junction), <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{{j}0}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-70-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-70">C_{{j}0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is junction capacitance at zero bias, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{j}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-71-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-71">V_{{j}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the voltage across the junction, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Phi</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-72-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-72">\Phi</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the junction potential. The small-signal approximation for the junction currents at zero bias is given as <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_2d">[23]</a> <disp-formula id="deqn14" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{&amp;I(V_{{j}})\approx 2\left({1\over R_{{j}}} \!+\! \jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\right) V_{{j}} \!+\!\left({\alpha\over R_{{j}}} \!+\!{\jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\gamma\over\Phi} \right) V_{{j}}^2.\qquad&amp;\hbox{(14)} }</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-73-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-73">\eqalignno{&amp;I(V_{{j}})\approx 2\left({1\over R_{{j}}} \!+\! \jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\right) V_{{j}} \!+\!\left({\alpha\over R_{{j}}} \!+\!{\jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\gamma\over\Phi} \right) V_{{j}}^2.\qquad&amp;\hbox{(14)} }</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{&amp;I(V_{{j}})\approx 2\left({1\over R_{{j}}} \!+\! \jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\right) V_{{j}} \!+\!\left({\alpha\over R_{{j}}} \!+\!{\jmath \omega C_{{j}0}\gamma\over\Phi} \right) V_{{j}}^2.\qquad&amp;\hbox{(14)} }</span></span></disp-formula>The first term represents the current of a normal (voltage independent) capacitor, which does not produce any modulated voltages, whereas the second term generates mixing products. Hence, the modulated current (at the first side band) of the equivalent current generator for the Schottky diode is <disp-formula id="deqn15" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\displaylines{I_{{dM}} = 2\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}} R_{{a}}}S_{{ja}} S_{{jm}}V_{0}\left({\alpha\over R_{{j}}} +{\jmath\omega C_{{j}0}\over\Phi}\right)\hfill\cr junction voltages due to the oscillator across diodes 1 and 2, respectively. For simplicity, we assume that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{jm}1}= V_{{jm}2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-78-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-78">V_{{jm}1}= V_{{jm}2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Thus, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">I_{{dM}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-79-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-79">I_{{dM}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the nonlinear term of <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn14" href="#deqn14" class="fulltext-link">(14)</a>. The oscillator output voltage is represented by a voltage source <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{\rm osc}}= V_0 \cos (\omega_{{\rm osc}}t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-80-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-80">V_{{\rm osc}}= V_0 \cos (\omega_{{\rm osc}}t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{0}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-81-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-81">V_{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the oscillator ac output voltage amplitude and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\omega_{{\rm osc}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-82-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-82">\omega_{{\rm osc}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is oscillator angular frequency. As can be seen from <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-83-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-83">Z_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> consists of an RF choke and a dc block capacitor. The voltage at <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f = f_{{\rm RF}}-f_{{\rm osc}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-84-Frame" Sensor Response At the Modulation Frequency</h3><p>By substituting <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn12" href="#deqn12" class="fulltext-link">(12)</a> and <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn16" href="#deqn16" class="fulltext-link">(16)</a> into <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn18" href="#deqn18" class="fulltext-link">(18)</a> we get an equation for the backscattered voltage of the sensor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{{a},{s}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-91-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-91">V_{{a},{s}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (across the antenna) at the lower sideband as a function of input power <disp-formula id="deqn19" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{&amp; V_{{a},{s}}= {1\over\pi}\sqrt{2P_{{\rm in}} R_{{a}}}S_{{ja}}S_{{jm}}Z_{{N}} I_{{\rm DC}}\cr &amp; \qquad\;\;\cdot\left[\left(I_{1({j},{\rm DC)}} \!+\! I_{2({j},{\rm DC)}}\right)\left({R_{{j}} R_{{L}}(V_{{\rm DC}})\over 2R_{{j}} \!+\!R_{L}(V_{{\rm DC}})}\right)\right]\cr &amp; \qquad\;\;\cdot\left[\left({1\over 2} \!+\! {C_{2}\over C_{1}\!+\!
