I_{2({j},{\rm DC)}}\right)\left({R_{{j}} R_{{L}}(V_{{\rm DC}})\over 2R_{{j}} \!+\!R_{L}(V_{{\rm DC}})}\right)\right]\cr &amp; \qquad\;\;\cdot\left[\left({1\over 2} \!+\! {C_{2}\over C_{1}\!+\! C_{S}} \right) \left({\alpha\over R_{j}}\!+\!{\jmath\omega C_{{j}0}\over\Phi}\right) \left(\cos(\omega_{0}\!-\!\omega_{{\rm osc}})t\right)\right]\cr &amp;&amp; \hbox{(19)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_{{L}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-93-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-93">R_{{L}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the input resistance of the oscillator and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{{S}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-94-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-94">C_{{S}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the capacitance of the sensing element, which in this case is connected parallel to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_1</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-95-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-95">C_1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Moreover, the resistance is assumed constant although in practice it depends on the voltage <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref33" id="context_ref_33_2e">[33]</a>. This result can be used to describe the backscattered power of the sensor and also to predict the sensor backward read-out distance.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2f"><h3>F. Link Budget Calculation for Communication</h3><p>Let us derive the read-out distance of the sensor. The power received by the sensor located at a distance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">r</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-96-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-96">r</script></tex-math></inline-formula> from the reader is <disp-formula id="deqn20" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{{s}} = P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \left({\lambda\over 4\pi r}\right)^2 \eqno{\hbox{(20)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-97-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-97">P_{{s}} = P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \left({\lambda\over 4\pi r}\right)^2 \eqno{\hbox{(20)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">P_{{s}} = P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \left({\lambda\over 4\pi r}\right)^2 \eqno{\hbox{(20)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{{t}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-98-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-98">P_{{t}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the power transmitted by the reader, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{r}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-99-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-99">G_{{r}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the reader antenna gain (to the direction of the sensor), <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{{s}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-100-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-100">G_{{s}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the sensor antenna gain, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-101-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-101">\lambda</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the wavelength.</p><p>There are two conditions that must be fulfilled in order to read the sensor. First, the sensor must receive enough power to turn on its oscillator. Second, the response signal received by the reader must be above the noise floor of the receiver. These two conditions give two read-out ranges that can be distinct, forward <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(r_{{f}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-102-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-102">(r_{{f}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and backward <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(r_{{b}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-103-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-103">(r_{{b}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> read-out ranges. The forward read-out range can be obtained by requiring that the power received by the sensor [as given in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn20" href="#deqn20" class="fulltext-link">(20)</a>] exceeds the threshold power <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(P_{\rm th})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-104-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-104">(P_{\rm th})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> required to turn on the oscillator. Similarly, the backward read-out range is obtained by requiring that the signal received by the reader is above the noise floor. The read-out distance of the sensor is limited by the smaller one of the forward and backward distances and is given as <disp-formula id="deqn21" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">r = \mathop{\min}\limits_{{P_{\rm th}=P_{s}} \atop{r_{f} = r_{b}}}\cases{r_{f} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi} \sqrt{{P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}}\over P_{\rm th}}} &amp; $({\hbox{forward}})$\cr r_{b} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi}{\sqrt{\Gamma_{{m}} P_{{\rm th}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \over P_{{N}}}} &amp; $({\hbox{backward}})$} \eqno{\hbox{(21)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-105-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-105">r = \mathop{\min}\limits_{{P_{\rm th}=P_{s}} \atop{r_{f} = r_{b}}}\cases{r_{f} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi} \sqrt{{P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}}\over P_{\rm th}}} &amp; $({\hbox{forward}})$\cr r_{b} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi}{\sqrt{\Gamma_{{m}} P_{{\rm th}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \over P_{{N}}}} &amp; $({\hbox{backward}})$} \eqno{\hbox{(21)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">r = \mathop{\min}\limits_{{P_{\rm th}=P_{s}} \atop{r_{f} = r_{b}}}\cases{r_{f} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi} \sqrt{{P_{{t}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}}\over P_{\rm th}}} &amp; $({\hbox{forward}})$\cr r_{b} = \displaystyle {\lambda\over 4\pi}{\sqrt{\Gamma_{{m}} P_{{\rm th}}G_{{r}}G_{{s}} \over P_{{N}}}} &amp; $({\hbox{backward}})$} \eqno{\hbox{(21)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{{N}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-106-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-106">P_{{N}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the receiver sensitivity, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{{m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-107-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-107">\Gamma_{{m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the modulation factor, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">r_{f}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-108-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-108">r_{f}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">r_{b}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-109-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-109">r_{b}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents the sensor forward and backward read-out distance, respectively.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Experiment and Simulation</h2></div><p>UHF RFID frequencies vary in each country depending on particular frequency regulations. The worldwide UHF RFID band is 860â960 MHz. In order to validate the theoretical model in practice, an example sensor design is fabricated and measured. The sensor is designed to operate at 844.5 MHz, and the purpose of the design is to validate the model used rather than to design a sensor for any particular application. Moreover, Schottky diodes are used as rectifier and modulator, and frequency has little influence on their rectification and mixing characteristics. Therefore, our experiment demonstrates the concept also in frequencies allocated to UHF RFID in different countries.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. Rectifier and Modulator</h3><p>The rectifier and modulator prototype is implemented with lumped circuit elements soldered on a printed circuit board (PCB) made from a Rogers RT/Duroid 5870 substrate <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(\varepsilon_r= 2.33, \tan \delta = 0.0012)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-110-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-110">(\varepsilon_r= 2.33, \tan \delta = 0.0012)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. A lumped capacitor and inductor are used as matching elements and two zero-bias Schottky diodes as the rectifier and modulator. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5</a> shows a photograph of the prototype.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 5. </b><fig><p>Photograph of the prototype rectifier and modulator implemented with lumped elements soldered on the PCB.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The rectifier and modulator are simulated with ADS (ADS Design Environment, Santa Clara, CA, USA. [Online]. Available: <a target="_blank" href="http://www.agilent.com/">http://www.agilent.com/</a>) using the harmonic-balance simulation. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 6</a> shows the rectifier and modulator simulation model. In simulation, discrete passives are modeled with S-parameters provided by the component manufacturers. The component values used in the calculations, simulations, and measurements are listed in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ II</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 6. </b><fig><p>Schematic circuit of the rectifier and modulator simulation model with microstrip lines. All microstrips have the same width, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">W = 1</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-111-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-111">W = 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and all dimensions are in millimeters.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TableÂ II </b>Parameters of the rectifier and modulator used in calculations, simulations, and measurements<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-2-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>The rectified voltage is measured with a digital multimeter (HP 3478A) and the modulated voltage at one sideband is measured with a spectrum analyzer (Anritsu MS2683A). A continuous wave (CW) is generated by a signal generator (Rohde &amp; Schwarz SML-03) and fed to the rectifier through a circulator (Voyantic V2.0). For testing purposes, the modulation voltage is generated with a signal generator (Rohde &amp; Schwarz SML-06) at 150 kHz and fed to the oscillation port. The signal reflected from the modulator is distinguished with a circulator and detected by the spectrum analyzer receiver.</p><p>The simulated and measured rectified voltage as a function of input frequency is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 7</a>. The input power is kept constant (19 dBm) and the load resistance is 240 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{k}}\Omega</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-112-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-112">{\hbox{k}}\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The simulated and measured curves align almost perfectly at frequencies below 840 MHz, but at higher frequencies the simulated voltage is approximately 10 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu{\hbox{V}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-113-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-113">\mu{\hbox{V}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> higher than the measured one. The offset between the simulated and measured curves can be caused, for example, by the accuracy of the printing and etching process and component variations.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-7-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-7-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 7. </b><fig><p>Rectified dc voltage as a function of frequency. Input power to the rectifier is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{\rm in}=-19</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-114-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-114">P_{\rm in}=-19</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dbm.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>As can be seen in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 7</a>, the circuit exhibits the maximum dc voltage at the input frequency of 844.5 MHz. In general, nonlinear properties of diodes change slowly with the frequency, but the peak is due to the matching between the diodes and antenna.</p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 8</a> shows the calculated, simulated, and measured dc voltage of the rectifier at different input power levels. The CW frequency is 844.5 MHz and the output load is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">R_L = 0.5~{\hbox{M}}\Omega</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-115-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-115">R_L = 0.5~{\hbox{M}}\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, which is the approximate input impedance of the oscillator obtained from the simulation. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 8</a> exhibits a good agreement between the analytical, simulated, and calculated curves at small power levels. The calculated curve would naturally fail at high power levels as the model is derived assuming small-signal analysis.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-8-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-8-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 8. </b><fig><p>Rectified dc voltage as a function of input power. The CW frequency is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">f_{\rm CW} = 844.5</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-116-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-116">f_{\rm CW} = 844.5</script></tex-math></inline-formula> MHz.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The backscattered power of the modulator is studied by sweeping the oscillation output voltage and keeping the oscillation frequency and input power constant. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 9</a> shows the analytical, simulated, and measured backscattered power of the modulator as a function of output voltage of the oscillator. The RF input power is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{19}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-117-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-117">{-}{\hbox{19}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm and the oscillation frequency is 150 kHz. The simulated and measured curves agree well and they correspond to the analytical results. Note that small deviation in analytical results is due to component losses that are neglected when deriving <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn18" href="#deqn18" class="fulltext-link">(18)</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 9. </b><fig><p>Backscatter power as a function of oscillator output voltage at input power level of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{19}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-118-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-118">{-}{\hbox{19}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dbm.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b"><h3>B. Oscillator</h3><p>The oscillator circuit is implemented using the BPS stripboard-3u. An ALD110800A matched pair MOSFET transistor is used in the oscillator, whose prototype is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 10(a)</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 10. </b><fig><p>(a) Photograph of the prototype oscillator implemented by using the BPS stripboard-3u. (b) Schematic circuit of the oscillator simulation model.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 10(b)</a> represents a schematic circuit of the oscillator simulation model used in ADS. A SPICE (LEVEL 2) model is used for the transistor in the simulation. The component values used in the simulation, calculation, and measurement are listed in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table3" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ III</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table3"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TableÂ III </b>Parameters of the oscillator used in calculations, simulations, and measurements<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-3-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>The oscillator is tested by measuring its output ac voltage and oscillation frequency as a function of input dc voltage supplied by an Agilent E3631A dc voltage supply, and the waveform is captured with an oscilloscope (Tektronix TDS 1002).</p><p>The calculated, simulated, and measured oscillation frequency as a function of the input dc voltage is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig11" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 11</a>. Calculations based on <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn7" href="#deqn7" class="fulltext-link">(7)</a> agree well with the simulation, while the measured frequency is approximately 19% smaller. We assume that the deviation between the measured and simulated curves occurs because of the measurement probe used to measure the oscillation frequency. The probe is a capacitive load to the oscillator, lowering its frequency. Note that the small deviation between the calculated and simulated frequency is due to parasitic capacitance of the MOSFET. An ideal MOSFET is assumed in the calculated curve.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig11"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-11-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-11-small.gif" alt="Figure 11" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 11. </b><fig><p>Measured, simulated, and calculated oscillation frequency as a function of input dc supply voltage.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig12" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 12</a> shows the measured, simulated, and calculated output voltage of the oscillator at different input dc voltages. According to analytical and simulated results, oscillation is sustained when the input dc voltage is above 69.3 and 73.7 mV, respectively, whereas the measured turn-on voltage is 82.6 mV. Thus, the oscillator turn-on voltage calculated according to <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn8" href="#deqn8" class="fulltext-link">(8)</a> corresponds well to the simulated and measured turn-on voltages. Later, oscillator turn-on voltage will be considered as the threshold voltage of the sensor.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig12"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-12-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-12-small.gif" alt="Figure 12" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 12. </b><fig><p>Measured, simulated, and calculated oscillator output voltage as a function of input dc supply voltage.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3c"><h3>C. Sensor</h3><p>The sensor prototype is implemented by integrating all three parts together. A photograph of the sensor prototype is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig13" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 13</a>. We demonstrate passive wireless sensor functionality by adding in parallel to capacitor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_1</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-119-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-119">C_1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> a trimmer capacitor to represent a capacitive sensor element. The capacitance of the trimmer capacitor for different rotation angles is measured with an HP 4192A LF impedance analyzer.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig13"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-13-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-13-small.gif" alt="Figure 13" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 13. </b><fig><p>Photograph of the sensor prototype where a trimmer capacitor is used to represent a sensor.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig14"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-14-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-14-small.gif" alt="Figure 14" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 14. </b><fig><p>Backscatter power responses of the sensor as a function of sensor input power levels.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>In the wired measurement, the CW is generated with a signal generator and fed through a circulator to the sensor like in the rectifier and modulator measurement. The reflected signal is separated with a circulator and fed to the spectrum analyzer.</p><p>In addition to the wired measurement, the sensor response is measured wirelessly. The wireless measurement is carried out with the Voyantic measurement system (Hand carry kit, Voyantic Ltd., Espoo, Finland. [Online]. Available: <a target="_blank" href="http://www.voyantic.com/">http://www.voyantic.com/</a>) and the sensor is connected to an ultra-wideband patch antenna (FXUB.66 maximus ultra-wide band antenna, Taoglas antenna solution. [Online]. Available: <a target="_blank" href="http://www.taoglas.com/">http://www.taoglas.com/</a>). In the measurement, the reader antenna and sensor are placed 8 m away from each other. The Voyantic measurement system was controlled with LabVIEW-based software.</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig15"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-15-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-15-small.gif" alt="Figure 15" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 15. </b><fig><p>Sensor oscillation frequencies of the sensor as a function of sensor capacitance.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
As can be seen from <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn19" href="#deqn19" class="fulltext-link">(19)</a>, an analytical equation for the backscattered voltage is derived. However, the sensor read-out distance is always measured from the backscattered power. Thus, the backscattered voltage is converted into backscattered power using the well-known formula <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P = V^2/R</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-120-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-120">P = V^2/R</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. In this case, antenna impedance is matched to 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Omega</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-121-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-121">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Measured and calculated backscattered power as a function of input power level are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig14" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 14</a>. According to the calculation, the threshold power of the sensor is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{28}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-122-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-122">{-}{\hbox{28}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm, whereas it is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{22}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-123-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-123">{-}{\hbox{22}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm in the measurement. There is an offset between the measured and calculated curves, but they exhibit similar shape. This offset can be due to the input resistance of the oscillator. In the calculation, a 0.5-<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{M}}\Omega</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-124-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-124">{\hbox{M}}\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input resistance was assumed, although it is in reality voltage dependent and may be different from the assumed value. In addition, the oscillator and modulator input impedances consist of capacitance, inductance, and resistance. Complex impedance could be used as a load for better fit.<p></p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table4"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TableÂ IV </b>Calculated link budget of a wireless RFID sensor interrogated with a reader device (<span class="MathJax_Preview">f = 844.5~ {\hbox{MHz}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-125-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-125">f = 844.5~ {\hbox{MHz}}</script>)<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-table-4-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
The wired, wireless, and calculated frequency offset is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig15" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 15</a>. The slope of the calculated frequency offset slightly differs from the wired and wireless frequency offsets. The deviation is likely caused by the fact that the capacitance value of the trimmer capacitor may change when it is connected to the sensor circuit due to the other sensor components and parasitic effects. Note that the small deviation between wired and wireless measurement is due to the power level received by the sensor. In the wired measurement, the input power level is kept constant at <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{19}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-126-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-126">{-}{\hbox{19}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm, but in the wireless measurement, power received by the sensor varies due to interference and multipath propagation. In the present case, the corresponding power transmitted by the reader is approximately 27 dBm and the backscattered power received by the reader is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{73}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-127-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-127">{-}{\hbox{73}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm. The read-out distance in the wireless experiment was 12 m. The corresponding link budget calculation is presented in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table4" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ IV</a>.<p></p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig16"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-16-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/7177154/7138648/7138648-fig-16-small.gif" alt="Figure 16" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 16. </b><fig><p>Measured received power in the sensor and reader as a function of distance. The transmitted power of the reader antenna was 27 dbm.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7138648/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig16" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 16</a> shows the received power in the sensor and in the reader. Sensor sensitivity or power-up threshold of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{22}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-128-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-128">{-}{\hbox{22}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm and reader sensitivity of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{75}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-129-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-129">{-}{\hbox{75}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> dBm are also shown. In this measurement, the sensor response is measured by sweeping the transmitted power of the reader antenna. During the measurement, the sensor is placed 30 cm away from the reader antenna in the anechoic chamber. Finally, sensor forward and backward read-out distance is calculated accordingly to <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn21" href="#deqn21" class="fulltext-link">(21)</a>. As can be seen from <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig16" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 16</a>, when the prototype sensor is interrogated with a reader with 27-dBm transmit power, 7.7-dB antenna gain, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{-}{\hbox{75}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-130-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-130">{-}{\hbox{75}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-dBm sensitivity, and when the sensor is equipped with an antenna whose gain is 2.7 dBi, the detection distance of the sensor would be 14 m at 844.5 MHz.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>This paper has discussed an approach that can be used to harness the sensing capabilities of RFID. The presented concept has been analyzed theoretically and verified with simulations and measurements. Moreover, theoretical equations for various properties of the sensor RF parts and oscillator are derived and realized in practice.</p><p>A passive wireless sensor using a trimmer capacitor as a sensing element has been demonstrated to have a read-out distance of at least 14 m at 844.5 MHz. The experiments demonstrate a very good agreement between the measured and theoretically derived performance of the sensor. The given theoretical models can be applied to predict the sensor performance without carrying out any particular measurements.</p><p>Future work includes measuring the oscillator and modulator input impedance for optimizing the sensor performance. In addition, a sensor element will be attached to the oscillator circuit for measuring an external quantity and it will also be integrated into an existing RFID tag in order to enable the features of ID and the possibility to measure an external quantity with larger read-out distance.</p></div>
</div></div>
</div><!-- end ngIf: ft.sectionText -->

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>
